Goats are not primarily raised for their meat; they are usually kept for milking. Then, when they are too old for milking, they are slaughtered for meat, which doesn't yield much. Moreover, goat milk isn't as widely consumed as one might think. Perhaps it's too fatty for some people's preferences, so it's often used for making cheese, a process that takes longer and costs more. In fact, total global goat milk production was estimated at 18.7 million tonnes in 2017, whereas cow's milk production reached around 549 million metric tons in 2023. This makes cow's milk more profitable due to higher sales and lower costs. Additionally, in some countries, cows are considered holy and not used for food, which is why goats are used instead.

Actual commonality of the species should count for more than level rates and hatch times. We're asking what pokemon do they eat not what pokemon they should theoretically eat. Magikarp is top tier. Pidgey is much more common than Hoothoot. Psyduck is also more common and nearly the same size.  If we're dedicated to farming then Gyarados gives excellent yield at 500 lb each, which can be grown from common magikarp. Tentacool is like a 100 lb squid too but it's possible it's poisonous.
Magikarp is probably one of the top choices. In East Asia, carp are highly prized fish, and as you pointed out, they are very fast reproducing and growing, and fish don't need to be farmed you can just let them reproduce in the wild.
Chickens are only efficient to raise if your society produces excess grain to feed them. Cows goats and sheep eat grass, which humans can't eat and grows in regions where crop agriculture is hard. I'd argue that they are actually more efficient food sources in this context.
Culture plays a much bigger part than you're willing to give credit to. We aren't avoiding eating goats because they'd cost more. Beef is noticeably more expensive than either pork or chicken but we keep eating it because it's important in our culture and because our ancestors passed down cattle farming techniques and beef based cookery. We eat a lot less pork in America because there's a noticeable number of Jews and Muslims. Fish, especially freshwater fish, is very little consumed because Americans like convenient food and don't bother picking bones out of their meat.
Just because something is edible does always means it is eaten. For example zebra has lot of meat but isnt harvested cause it is hard to domesticate. So weak pokemons are more likely to be food source.
Just because a pokemon is heavy doesn't mean it has more meat (muscles).  Size of the pokemon should also taken into consideration.

Livestock are not slaughtered for meat as adults. When "mature" livestock are slaughtered for meat, they are not mature in the sense of being adults. They are not biologically adults, nor are they adults by human standards, as they are the equivalent of minors. They are what's called "sexually mature", which means they have reached puberty. They're adolescents, not adults. Beef cattle reach puberty at around 13 - 15 months, but are slaughtered at around 18 months, which is roughly equivalent to a 15-year-old human.  This is not to say that there isn't adult livestock meat on the market. When dairy cows are killed when they stop producing milk, their meat is sold. That is adult beef. But that is a byproduct of the dairy industry. Dairy beef, especially that of adults, is not the kind people prefer. People prefer adolescent beef from beef breeds.

The only PokÃ©mon that I think are not animals are actually the artificial ones, like Magnemite, Voltorb, Porygon, Magearna etc., those that are confirmed man-made or resemble robots enough that they can be considered non-animals. Not sure about Koffing/Weezing or Grimer/Muk, since those also seem to be man-made but aren't robots either. They're more like mutated animals. I like the theory that Grimer are corrupted Ditto, which in turn are failed clones of Mew, which is definitely an animal. I do consider slime PokÃ©mon animals, as slimes (the creature) in many fantasy universes are considered animals (or at least creatures, you get it, right?), so Gulpin/Swalot and other slimes like that still count as animals imo. Also unsure about Trubbish/Garbodor, maybe they're in the same boat as Koffing/Weezing and Grimer/Muk (minus the corrupted Ditto part). Regardless, almost all PokÃ©mon, whether plant, mineral or animal, are actually animals, and only a handful of them are robots. I do know that apparently Fomantis/Lurantis are supposed to be a plant resembling a mantis, thus doing a switcheroo on the orchid mantis being the opposite of that, but really, there are no plants in the real world that can walk around on their own, nor think or make choices, while animals can. So, logically/realistically, Fomantis/Lurantis are indeed actual animals and not plants. Even Ghost PokÃ©mon are animals that simply use the power of antimatter to be able to transcend through objects, but since they have genders and lay eggs just like Eevee or Bellsprout (plant egg, really?), it means they are animals.  Basically, what I can say is, if a PokÃ©mon can naturally lay an egg, it's an animal. If it doesn't have a gender and isn't legendary (coz gods won't breed for you puny humans), then its existence as an animal can be put into question. Since Ditto will have to lay the egg for that PokÃ©mon then, and since Ditto is an animal, naturally it lays eggs, even if it's an egg containing a non-animal like a Magnemite or a Bronzor. Of course, there are exceptions to this like Staryu which is clearly an animal, but genderless, but with these cases, simply use your common sense and knowledge for the answer. The gender and natural egg laying ability thing is merely to determine whether a plant/mineral/robot/non-animal-looking-thing is an animal or not. I know this video was a while ago, but Mat, you really should do more work on your Pokemon theories. I know the amount of work that you do for your theories, and I'm sure you do lots of work here too, but the number of mistakes in this video is just unbelievable.  You said Garchomp, but you showed a Gabite picture. I really don't know how you made this mistake, since it's not like it's that hard to find a Garchomp picture, and Gabite pictures shouldn't be popping up that often when looking for Garchomp ones.  You say that evolving your Pokemon wouldn't be worth it for farmers, but why would it not be? It doesn't take THAT much effort to train a Pokemon to evolve it, and there are even methods that you can use to raise a Pokemon's level without having to battle with it, and evolving Pokemon most often gets you a much larger Pokemon to slaughter.  I would harp on the pronunciation of Pokemon, but that's not all that important, and you didn't make any egregious mistakes in pronunciation like pronouncing Golisopod as Guh-lee-so-pod.  Mudbray and Mudsdale are mentioned as eating dirt and mud. Animals that eat dirt and mud for their nutrients typically have very poor meat. And while you COULD eat Mudsdale for its eat if you have to, there are much better options for meat in the same area. In fact, Miltank, which you opted to replace with Mudbray/Mudsdale, are more efficient because they produce milk and meat, and they have more meat sooner. As soon as Miltank hatch, they have more meat on their bones than Mudbray, which automatically means that Miltank is a better food source, even if you don't account for the fact that Mudbray/Mudsdale's meat would be pretty bad.  Finally, you ignore the fact that Pokemon, especially in the games, can reproduce REALLY fast. Like, thousands of times faster than real animals. At most, you're waiting half an hour or so between leaving your Pokemon to produce an egg and actually hatching that egg. Because of this, slaughtering the babies (don't take that out of context) would be far more efficient, especially for Pokemon that immediately hatch into their adult forms like Miltank, Tauros, and Bouffalant.
Got to take flavor into account. I imagine mudbray/mudsdale would taste like mud.

speaking as a chef, I don't know if I'd wanna eat a Mudbray or a Mudsdale for a few reasons.  1. more often than not animals will taste something like their environment. You see it with catfish (who can taste muddy), and Iberico ham (which taste nutty due to the acorns they eat). Granted that last one is from things they eat, but you get the point. Seeing as how both these pokemon are both ground types chances are they're gonna taste something like that. You don't base all your moves off of something if it doesn't impact you on a molecular level.   2. Even if that wasn't a factor there's still the issue that these are both working animals. These animals would actually be more worth wild as haulers/tillers/riding animals on the farm. You could actually make money just on giving Mudsdale rides to kids or renting them out to construction crews who need a lot of ground moved. Typically when you eat an animal you don't want it to be doing a lot of work. The more work it does the tougher the meat. That's why things such as Veal and Lamb are so prized (and before you mention it, no. it is not common practice anymore to chain these animals to the ground. That went the way of the Foie Gras force feed method). Typically you'd only eat your horse or muel if you had absolutely no other option; otherwise you'd use it strictly as a work pokemon.  Honestly, I think one of the best pokemon you could have as a reusable source of food is Slowpoke. It's tails grow back after a certain period of time so it might be worth it to just have a little pond area where 20 or 30 slowpoke just chill, and you just hack their tails off every now and then. They won't even realize its gone until after you've cleaned the wound and gone back inside, and even then they won't do much about it. I'd also definitely submit Magikarp into consideration since it does have a pretty quick hatch rate, and they won't give you much trouble until they reach level 25 which is hard enough to do with a magikarp when you actually ARE battling it out.   TLDR: Don't eat the fuckin mud horse cause it'll taste like mud and you'll have to cook it for 16hrs before you can start to chew it

Matpat, Matpat, Matpat,  I feel like this missed the mark on genetic compositions of said pokemon. Mudbray and Mudsdale are so heavy because they're ground types and partially composed of earth. Meaning you're going to have some pretty muddy steaks to chose from making it more expensive because it's a labor intensive butchering process. Pokedex entries mention that the mud caked on Mudsdale sets harder than stone, so the equipment alone to process one of them would be tedious. Also, it's not surprising that Wailord is so light considering it's the Float Whale pokemon, and depictions of them in games as wild pokemon showcase them floating along the surface of the water  As for the bird pokemon, Matpat missed the mark by choosing an owl as a livestock pokemon. Owls don't feed on grains and garbage like the other Pidgey clone birds, they eat rodents, in this case most likely Rattata. Now that you're feeding that "efficient" pokemon it's proper diet of other pokemon, the cost of raising it skyrockets. Not to mention that owls are not very meaty, and a majority of Hoothoot's weight is most likely going to be bone and organ. Going for it on weight alone, especially after bringing up the average weight of a chicken and knowing how much even one chicken feeds, was a flop.  Because Matpat based a majority of his final decision on weight, he dropped the ball. Pokedex weights go off of the data colllected from WILD pokemon. Not a pokemon that's been farm raised and fattened for slaughter. Calling back to my first paragraph, Mudbray/Mudsdale is only so heavy because it's made of dirt and rocks, and he'd be better off going for a pokemon more traditionally animalistic that could be fed a diet and maybe even selectively bred for meat and byproduct yields  The biggest thing the video missed was taking into account that different pokemon are native to different regions, meaning export costs have to be taken into account if you are lumping everything as one basic meat. Matpat even mentioned that different livestock is better in different parts of the world, and then completely failed to deliver a conclusion to that point, making instead a one-size-fits all answer. The video would have benefitted from a regional approach that takes into account the real life counterparts of said regions.  Hightailing of of the comment about regions, a good number of those regions are islands, and you can find fishermen in every single pokemon game. Meaning the most likely solution for a catch all meat is a fish, specifically a Magikarp. Carp is a real life edible fish and even in the pokemon anime it's been implied that they're eaten. They're also known as a weak pokemon so they pose less of a threat to someone than I'd say a majority of any other pokemon choice. Mudsdale is that heavy for a reason, and it will not hesitate to trample over a trainer.  In conclusion. Lazy shortcuts were made for the sake of a video that could have been much more interesting and while I know this comment is nowhere near a heavily reviewed script, these are still point that really should have been considered.

There is a massive point this video overlooks when it comes to optimal livestock: animal behavior and physiology, and the mutually beneficial nature of domestication.  Throughout our history, humans have hunted, fished, and trapped a huge variety of animals. But you ever wonder why we haven't domesticated *all* the prey we've sought out?  Quite simply, some animals are just not suited for human civilization.   Take deer in North America â€“ they're a fantastic food source (ruminates in general are great, since convert all that inedible grass into meat and milk), and their herd behavior means they're fine being kept in some numbers. However, they are INCREDIBLY agile, migrating animals. Up until recently, catching and containing them would have been impossible. Even then, convincing them to breed and raise young in captivity can be tricky.  Cows, sheep, and goats? They like to stay put, and they aren't quite as athletically inclined. Goats do climb, though they are small enough that keeping their climbing contained isn't too difficult. They also do great with large herds and close quarters, as huddling together was already part of their survival strategy pre-domestication.  Even when it comes to companion animals, there's a massive difference between a domesticated vs. tamed pet. Besides generations of selective breeding required for domestication, some animals will take much differently to it.  Dogs are ideal animal companions to humans due to similar social structures and diets. Similarly, cats thrive in our care due to their dual predator-prey nature â€“ we keep away bigger predators while offering an easy food with the pests we attract. The now-feral pigeons you see in major cities are the result of escaped or released domestic birds thriving so well in human society, they just stuck around.  Finally, it's important to note that carnivores are generally bad food sources, while omnivores can be dicey. Predatory animals offer boast a higher caloric density over prey animals, but also greater risk â€“ both because they've concentrated things like parasites, heavy metals, etc. from the rest of the food chain. This is why eating predatory fish is generally something that needs to be limited, as they have incredibly high concentrations of mercury. Simultaneously, carnivores cannot often be kept together in great numbers. A fair amount of predators are also solitary. It's not uncommon to see aggression and/or cannibalism when kept together.  All of this is to say: just consider the feats some of these Pokemon are capable of. Magikarp might *seem* like an easy source of protein, but what happens when Friday's fish fry becomes Saturday's localized monsoon because part of the swarm evolved? How much effort would it take to slaughter a *giant, flying psychic bird of prey* like Noctowl on an industrial scale? What processing, tools, and funding is necessary for things like Fire-type Pokemon to be safe for handling?  All-in-all, I think this comes down to three points that make a truly ideal Pokemon livestock: 1. Pokemon that like to group together and breed easily, 2. Pokemon with low base stats and few-to-no evolutions, and  3. Pokemon with gentle temperaments. It is for this reasons that I propose that just like in many parts of the real world, Bug-type Pokemon are a massive food source for a lot of people. Paras and Parasect seem like a potentially popular option, as they're abundant and become more docile upon evolution. Humans are fond of both cordyceps mushrooms and arthropods like shrimp, too, so two food sources in one. As for industrial-scale farming: Luvdisc, Wobbuffet, Chansey line, and the Jigglypuff line are probably the best livestock Pokemon. Seriously. Fish farming is a huge industry, and Luvdisc is one of the weakest, most easily caught Pokemon. Seems easy to maintain on a farm. Wobbuffet takes ages to level up, but evolves from Wynaut at a relatively early Level 15. They're also just... really dumb, but incredibly hardy. Definitely something you want for livestock.  Chansey *also* produces eggs that are apparently edible on the spot, and is a very sociable Pokemon. I imagine it would mostly be an egg layer, but they're HUGE Pokemon. Lots of meat for the work of chipping through that HP. Jigglypuff is a concern with Sing, but that's easy to counteract with earplugs, and doubles as an incredible fur/leather producer with super soft fur and extra flexible skin.  And that's my theory.

Could you please PLEASE bake a salt cake? In master chef season 5 ep 18, Leslie supposedly baked a salt cake that cost him the round and his chance to win. How ever it is a hotly debated decision bc not only did the show not show such incident (like they would normally do) , but the judges didnâ€™t spit it out or anything. The behavior of everyone was off . Even Leslie, when he had to admit it. So maybe it was a hoax created by the show. So my challenge to you is to bake a salt cake and test the viewersâ€™ theory . "A single rotisserie Hoothoot will feed your entire family for a week."  Even discounting the equines as potentially only weighing that much due to inedible earthen biology, the hoothoot seems like it needs to factor into the cost side of the 'pokemon trainer profits' equation. Because if something takes roughly the same effort to raise as chickens for multiple times the meat, basic economics says that the resulting meat should be cheaper at market.  Given that pokemon also lower the manpower requirements for a lot of quality of life matters, I think this would tilt pokemon training solidly into 'reasonable' paycheck range, especially if, like gym members, you specialize in one type of pokemon that may very well have tradework value.  Incidentally, this video also accidentally answers many of the questions surrounding the existence of the 'human-like' egg group.  With the genetic similarities presented in the "humans are pokemon" video and the Joy connection, it seems obvious that the ones that are closest to humans are classified that way in part to remove them as food options under a wider umbrella of cannibalism.  After all, if there's breeding viability, many people count it as the same species on that alone. Not that that makes a lot of sense with egg groups or omnibreeders in the mix.

1 thing on the final summary: This entirely ignores the possibility of selective breeding. This is like comparing wild game to farmed animals, which can defo change how things look. (Remember, the games focus on wild-caught cockfighting animals. Not farm simulator)  Often times the animals we hunt, and the animals we domesticate don't always line up. Not many people hunt wild boar or cattle, yet pork and beef are staples of many cuisines.   So it may be entirely possible that some other form of domesticated bird pokemon is more common in grocery stores, where hoothoot are a common aim for hunters  Sidenote: Chancy may also be a big egg producer in the world. The only problem I have with this video (aside from when MatPat said Garchomp and Gabite showed up on screen) is that he didn't read up on Mudbray's and Mudsdale's diet from their Pokedex entries, which mainly state that they get their nutrients from mud. The problem with this is that animals that eat mud and dirt exclusively have very poor meat, for example mudcatfish, they bottom feed off of muddy ground underwater and end up with poor tasting meat since their nutrients come from the mud. Having something with very poor tasting meat quality as your main source of red meat isn't very appealing, so alternatives would definitely be chosen for that food role.

In theory, its plausible, but i do hold one major complaint on this. It's not region specific. You make mention of cows vs goats in real world farming around the world, but you don't do the same for the 8 regions of Pokemon.  Take Kanto and Johto for example, they would not naturally have access to Skiddo milk, but they have an abundance of Miltank milk, and since these two regions are quite literally a single river apart, they easily have shared resources, but Hoenn, Sinnoh, Unova, Kalos, Alola and Galar are all very far from each other and it very well couldn't share the same ecosystem. (Heck, Alola alone has 4 ecosystems to keep an eye on, all very diverse and demanding different stock of available resources.)   Across the table, the Pokemon listed are good options, but they're far from ideal in practice. Not to sound totally nitpicky about it, but I would not consider this an accurate list unless it's verifiable that all Pokemon listed can be found and easily raised in every region. Have to remember the main reasons that chickens, pigs and cows are farmed is that before  modern technology there was only a very limited number of animals that could be domesticated. This is because of 4 main reasons, firstly the animal must be friendly, you could attempt to farm tigers but they will try and eat you. The worst a chicken can do it peck you kinda hard  Secondly they need to be feedable, everyone knows that pigs will eat anything you give them. You canâ€™t feed pandas that way they are too picky. You also canâ€™t farm pure carnivores purely because of thermodynamics, 10 pound of grass makes a pound of steak and 10 pounds of steak makes a pound of tiger. It just doesnâ€™t make sense to waste food you could eat to farm a second animal  Third they need to have a family hierarchy we can exploit if you catch and take the alpha horse all the others will fall in line (usually). Catch a zebra and the others donâ€™t care they travel in packs as a defence mechanism not as a family hierarchy   Finally they need to be sexually active and grow up fast. Pandas again canâ€™t be farmed because of this, without human intervention these guys would be extinct right now because their mating preferences are just too much. Whereas cows are sexually active when you put a bull into a field of cows they will all be pregnant by the end of the week. Growing up fast is critical as well, it takes a chicken ~18 weeks to be considered mature, elephants can take 5-7 years to reach sexual maturity  Obviously in todayâ€™s time the number of farmable animals has increased but there is still only a finite number that can be farmed, I would assume that these factors are the same in the Pokemon world. Taurus and Bouffalant would be great to farm but they are effectively tanks on hooves that will destroy and cage/barrier in its path

Jokes aside, I'm not sure that Nurse Joy being a Pokemon would make eating one more acceptable in the Pokemon world. In our world, a lot of people have strong feelings about eating animals like horses or dogs because they serve or help humans, and very few would be comfortable with the idea of eating other primates despite their not technically being humans because it's just too close for comfort. The idea of eating animals seen as intelligent makes people uncomfortable. Conversely, some people do engage in cannibalism, like the Donner Party for instance... so even if someone did choose eat a Nurse Joy, that act wouldn't necessarily prove she was a Pokemon. That person might well be a cannibal. Even if you do accept the argument that she's a Pokemon, I'm sure given how human she seems and how much she helps everyone, it would bother them at least as much as the idea of eating intelligent animals and service animals would bother us. With that out of the way, my own personal theory is that the diet of people in Pokemon skews towards seafood, because those are depicted as the least intelligent Pokemon, and resemble their real-world counterparts more closely. Also, Pokemon is from Japan, where it was traditionally the case that there were a lot of Buddhists comfortable with eating seafood but not any other kind of meat. That philosophy seems to be at least somewhat reflected in what Pokemon the anime talks about people potentially eating most often.

There is a reason humans have only only one true carnivore domesticated, cats, and that is solely because them eating the meat, rats, is their job. All other species are either obligate herbivores such as cattle and goats, eating tough plants we are unable to, or opportunistic omnivores willing to eat whatever we give them, even our table scraps, such as dogs and pigs. The reason why is that ten pounds of grass might make one pound of lamb, but it would take ten pounds of lamb to make one pound of wolf. For obvious reasons you just cut out the last step and eat the lamb! If you want to eat the animal, like Matpat mentions you would since he was selecting a meat bird, then he would want something much like a chicken, willing to eat grain or insects and a general omnivore if hungry enough, and they have an incredible feed efficiency. And beyond that, owls are slower breeders. The reason chickens are so incredible is that they breed so incredibly fast! The turn around on a rooster and a few hens is unbelievable! One thing to consider with miltank vs skidoo is miltank is always female, so 100% of the miltank eggs your daycare produces will be able to produce delicious milk while only 50% of skidoo eggs will hatch females. With cows this isnâ€™t a problem because the males can become delicious meat, but skidoo is a grass type PokÃ©mon and grass types probably occupy that awkward middle area where they arenâ€™t meat and non-PokÃ©mon plants are much easier to justify eating. Thereâ€™s also the question of what conditions are required to produce milk, IRL a cow has to have a baby to produce milk, but the PokÃ©mon world implies miltank are more like farming sim cows in that they produce milk constantly. By contrast the presence of baby skidoo suggests skidoo have to have had offspring recently in order to produce milk. Anyone whoâ€™s played the Harvest Moon/Story of Seasons series will be able to tell you cows that produce milk constantly without breeding are much more efficient than cows that have to be bred periodically to continue producing milk. That series only tried making cows realistic once.   Also the PokÃ©mon world has a strong regional cuisine culture dependent on whatever the native PokÃ©mon are, so while hoothoot might be the fowl of choice for Johto Kanto is more likely to go with pidgey or spearow.

Farming animals is only "profitable" because of the crazy amount of money the U.S government gives to farmers in the form of subsidies. Take that away and farmers would either be in the red or beef would cost roughly $35 to break even. Considering the amount of overhead cost, raising the animals for a few months to a few years to be big enough to produce meat with the amount of grain and water, general labor, and unforeseen costs (animals dying early, cost of grain increasing etc.) the math of producing  $0.97 per/lb for chicken, $1.69 per/lb for pork, and $1.64 is just not realistic if subsidies are not taken into consideration. Also very disingenuous to say we have a lot of land and water when we are literally going through continue water shortages in many parts of the U.S as well as outsourcing our farming to other country due to a shortage of land that can act as pasture from over farming. I love your channels MatPat but the due diligence on this video was just not done. Using one farmer as a source to base most of the info while ignoring the concept of subsidies is not going to cut it.

Sure, you'd need a Magmar to get them, since they ARE baby pokemon, but the parent wasn't considered in your analysis. Plus, being a fire type, it's probably already seasoned well. shouldve said ft. Game Theory
A big factor that really needs to be included as well, (though obviously can't since we can't try Pokemon meat ourselves), is the taste factor. Real animals have vastly different tastes depending on their species, as well as depending on their diets and living conditions. Fresh water and salt water fish taste very different for example. By extension it stands to reason that Pokemon would have a similar situation, but probably even more so since their elemental natures often include that element being a part of their body in some way or form. Mudsdale may taste very earthy for example, which is already a part of the reason some people don't like catfish meat in real life. While it's true that in poorer countries they would probably eat it regardless due to efficiency, other countries would likely try to replace it with something that tastes better but is still efficient, because people would be more willing to buy products that taste better so long as the price difference isn't too drastic.

Wow. That is an interesting collaboration. Matpat did make a video about Winnie the Pooh and his honey diet. Looking back, I think that could be a collaboration of Food Theory and Film Theory.   That is interesting to figure out which Pokemon would be the most viable food sources. I think the use of Pokeballs would help a lot with farming. In more modern times there are factory farms. Animals are crammed together in tight enclosed in buildings. This is a way to save on costs. Animals are cheep. Cages are expensive. So it is cost effective to stuff more animals into fewer cages. In real life, factory farms are horrifying. I feel so sorry for the poor animals. They are cramped up with no space, sunshine or fresh air. I think the Pokemon world is the one place where factory farming can work. Pokemon can go in Pokeballs, which saves a ton of space. There can be whole big buildings full of Pokeballs containing livestock. The Pokeballs would be packed tight together. It is like the Pokemon in Professor Oaks lab, except the don't get out much. A Pokemon would only be left out during meal times, so it can eat. The rest of the time, it stays in the ball. It doesn't get any excercise let alone battles. A sedentary life makes it easier to get fattened for eating. Maybe the farms can develop a way to transfer food inside a Pokeballs without getting the Pokemon out. Maybe there would also be a way to transfer waste out. People may say that this is cruel. They do have a point there. However this is a world where animal fighting is not only legal but is embraced by the culture. The animal rights ship has sailed long ago. Keeping Pokemon in Pokeballs is already a ubiquitous action. So farms can do more of that. At least the most greedy and uncaring farmers would figure out how to do factory farms for Pokemon. If Team Plasma found out, they would freak out.

I would like to argue something ignored by.. a fair chunk of the comments section.  Have you SEEN farm animals? They chase, sprint, headbutt, jump, splash in any puddles they find.. much of which is play-fighting.  Pokemon in the anime train amongst one another (even outside of true battles) and headbutt trees and rocks to grow stronger.  a fast enough leveling pokemon, given ample space and other pokemon to play with (""Battle"") wouldn't need much oversight from a farmer to evolve. While this doesn't disprove using a horse/donkey pokemon for meat, it does give more opportunity to use other pokemon as a food source.

Weight alone seems like a poor measure of how much meat you get out of a pokemon. How much of that weight is actually meat? Mudsdale may clock in at an impressive 2k pounds, but given it's height of 8 feet tall, it's almost as tall as a mature elephant, but with a fraction of the weight. It seems like most of that weight will be coming from it's robust and massive skeletal structure and there would be considerably less left for the meat. Given it's lean appearance and the amount of weight it needs to move around, the muscles would also most likely be very tough, and might not make for an enjoyable dining experience.

I feel like there are a few things that weren't considered here. For one, in the milk production Miltank is still going to be the best option. Alpha/Omega pokedex states that it can produce over 5 gallons a day. While not more than a regular dairy cow, they are much smaller at roughly 1/10 the weight.   As for eating meat, the weight doesn't equal a greater food source. For that to be true the muscle mass would scale as well, showing a larger and heavier pokemon. Mudsdale and mudbray are heavy sure, but we don't know if that's muscle, bone, or most likely straight up mud and rocks. Mudsdale and mudbray eat dirt for sustenance as well, meaning that breeding and farming them would be almost impossible, as dirt (unlike grass) doesn't just grow. Even if they are made of meat, they make it from dirt, meaning it would constantly dig out whatever land you had until it was just dead rocks. Not saying there aren't applications to this, just that in terms of food, it seems unlikely. (Also it'd probably taste rank and be incredibly tough)

i feel this ignores the fact of typing though. its assumed Mudsdale has predominant weight in its fur and legs due to dried clay. so accounting for weight doesnt work for all pokemon. unless the pokemon is labelled as a non material type, material tpes being rock, ground, steel, and ice, it can be assumed most of its weight most likely isnt "edible material". as for other factors if you then want to be realistic a pokemon like wailord, the Float whale pokemon, then also has even less, as its density mostly likely comes from having a massive air bladder and it doesnt have much material to eat either which lines up with its weight to size ratio. HootHoot while its also very heavy, you need to account for the fact by shape, its bones and large eyes make up probably a hefty majority of its body so even with a high weight category as a bird pokemon, probably only half of that would be edible material. finally, you need to account for all these pokemon's diets. we see almost all pokemon eating just standard pokefood, but all pokemon have a dietary restriction of some variety so to feed any "livestock" it would be most efficient to avoid any pokemon that are known to consume other pokemon. this unfortunately rules out alot of the bird pokemon as possible meat sources since many are birds of prey and thusly harder to feed efficiently as well. in the series itself seafood seems to be one of the most common protien sources though there are some red meats as well but they dont seem as prevalent.

There's one catch to that poultry answer, though: Owls are carnivorous, feeding on small mammals.  That not only makes them more expensive to raise but also more difficult to eat.  When measuring caloric efficiency for carnivores, you have to factor in the caloric efficiency of their prey, too.  Furthermore, carnivore meat isn't as easy to digest as herbivore meat.

I think there's another factor being overlooked here.  The diet of the potentially farmed animal and how efficient /that/ is.  An animal that eats hay and/or grass, like a cow, is easier to supply than an animal that itself primarily eats meat.  The Pokedex entries on Hoothoot doesn't discuss diet, but Noctowl is frequently referred to as a nighttime predator.  It's possible that the pokemon's diet changes during evolution; a lot of pokemon change a lot; but if the Hoothoot largely eats meat like regular owls, it'd be expensive to raise them because of diminishing returns.

One factor that goes overlooked here is the rate at which Pokemon lay eggs. You might get more meat per individual Pokemon faster on a HootHoot but if Pidgey lays eggs twice or 3 times as fast then the amount of meat per Pokemon matters less and could be outpaced fairly quickly. There's no real canon sources on the rate at which pokemon lay eggs but if it's analagous to real world animals, smaller prey animals will tend towards having more children, as to outpace the rate at which they're hunted by predators. Larger predators meanwhile have fewer, so there's not as much competition for food and water. Bug Pokemon, I think, are probably the best candidates in terms of efficiency, maybe grass, as they tend towards being smaller prey animals that depend on large colonies, while grass Pokemon have the advantage of being able to supplement large parts of their diets with photosynthesis while also potentially relying on large groups, making farming them much less costly in terms of feeding. Bug Pokemon in particular, if they ARE analagous to their real world counterparts, are also universally larger and more massive than the real thing, Caterpie and Weedle, for example, are about the size of a small dog, as are Paras and Parasect, though most of these are poisonous. Given the rate at which they multiply, the Nincada line seems like a good candidate for a staple food, the fact that when they evolve,  they produce an entire extra pokemon that could be used for meat (if Shedinja isn't just an empty shell in terms of meat), and that rare candies mean that evolution can be artificially accelerated. It would produce meat on an exponential growth curve. Though you can't ignore flavor as part of this discussion. If people don't like the taste of Nincada, Ninjask, or Shedinja meat, it won't be adopted as a staple food.  The best candidate, I think, would be the Combee line. Massive enough that a single one could produce a decent sized meal, reproduce in colonies, and likely taste like honeycomb given that they're both the bee AND the hive itself. Being shaped like honeycombs, they're also really efficient in terms of space, fitting together in the most efficient configuration possible so any farm specializing in Combees and Vespiqueens is going to be able to fit a LOT of them on a relatively small amount of land.

Hoothoot would probably not be the most efficient choice for poultry for one huge reason - the ecological pyramid. On the bottom are plants, which donâ€™t require a lot of energy - just the sun; above them are herbivores, which require the energy of the sun which goes into the plants they eat; and above them are carnivores which require the sun going into the plants going into the herbivores going into them. This usually has economic implications, and why we donâ€™t tend to eat a lot of carnivores. We donâ€™t really eat a lot of carnivores because it takes a lot of energy to feed them - and while chickens are capable of eating meat (theyâ€™ll go after mice), their major diet is cheap grain. After all, ten pounds of grain is far cheaper than ten pounds of pinky mice. So, assuming Hoothoot are similar to real world owls in their diet and can only survive on meat, that means a vegetarian pidgey-clone, or one that can survive on mostly plants,  is going to produce more meat for your investment. So out of the three that were narrowed down, one Hoothoot cannot produce as much meat for the same cost of being raised on Ratatta as the horde of Spearow (assuming a diet similar to sparrows, which can live off seeds, grains, and our garbage - like pigs) you can raise on the huge amount of grain available for the same price as Ratatta meat. So unless the return on meat from a single Hoothoot is just that much more than all those Spearow put together for the same cost of raising, Spearow makes the most economic sense. Sorry, Matt!

Great video, but a few things you didn't take into account First- in the real world, the main reason certain animals are farmed and others aren't, has to do with social structure. Animals that are highly social (cows, horses, chickens, goats) are much easier to domesticate and therefore farm than animals that aren't (consider how we can domesticate horses but not zebra. Zebra live in large herds, but don't have a social hierarchy) The other, less important thing you didn't mention is that many Pokemon are not made up of "meat" like a lot of ground or rock types are weird sentient rocks, and I think mudbray falls into this category of being ground type and most of its body weight being mud or rocks or something

This was a lot of fun ^^ I *will* point out that as Hoothoot is a predator, like any owl, they might not actuallybe such a natural choice for poultry farmers. You'd have to provide meat for them to eat, and enough to fatten them up. Furthermore, as a predator they would be far less docile and generally require much more work to handle (being a pokÃ©mon might mitigate this problem). Finally, (it just hit me while writing) as owls are solitary predators, you would either require a lot of land per Hoothoot or have to put in a lot of work to prevent conflict between them.   Basically: Unlike grass, mice aren't free.  Some species of PokÃ©mon display herd behaviour, making any species that doesnâ€™t, an inefficient source of meat.
It always surprises me how little Lamb/Mutton is eaten or even remembered as a meat source in the US unless it's a very expensive, high-end restaurant meal. Particularly with the abundant green space for raising large flocks the US has.   In the UK it's equal in popularity to beef or pork (and in certain areas more common). Especially when you think about Sunday roasts, mince and shanks.  Beef will always be the go-to for steak but ties with lamb for stews and pork is usually the preference for chops. But Shepard's pie, Irish stew, pasties, lamb roasts with mint sauce, lamb shank in red wine and lamb sausages are widely eaten as a regular part of the diet here.

This ignores some important factors with the milk, even if Miltank takes longer to grow to maturity, each Miltank almost definitely produces an absurd amount of milk more per day per Miltank than a Gogoat. One slight thing you missed accounting for regards the hatching of pokemon. You can cut the hatch times in half if you have a pokemon that has the Magma Armor or Flame Body abilities... and Steam Engine in gen 8... such as Slugma/Magcargo, or more appropriately, Talonflame.  It would make sense that if pokemon were to all come from eggs, farmers would also be raising families of tamed Talonflames that could sit on/nest with any eggs produced, thus hatching them twice as fast and allowing farmers to raise new livestock a lot quicker, meaning those ones like Chansey (with it's ~10k step counter) would be just as fast to hatch as Mudbray would without the extra heat.  To add to that, you mention about how farmers wouldn't be out evolving pokemon... but given how large some of these can get, Mudsdale in particular (which would be mostly strong muscle meat), it might be worth the effort.  You should also add in the idea of Bug pokemon. Insects are eaten, quite frequently, in the real world. If we're talking mere weight vs time/resources spent, I'd say that Ladyba (~4k steps hatch count, 10.8kg at hatch, and with a Fast levelling rate) would likely be a big candidate for most eaten... Ladian even moreso, as it more than triples in weight once evolved (though likely at least 1/3 of that will be inedible carapace), and only needs to be Lv18. Thus would require far less food and training to evolve, so more could be fed and raised at a single time.

While it may make up a comparatively small amount of western diets, PokÃ©mon is a Japanese property, and fish make up around 40% of protein in the average Japanese diet. (I haven't compared all fish Pokemon, Feebas and Goldeen come to mind as contenders, but went with Magikarp as a default) Considering that Magikarp have the fastest hatch rate and require little space and care (going off of real life fish farming processes and Magikarp jump of all games) I'd assume they make up a significant part of the market. Granted the average weight of 22lb/10kg isn't great, alongside the slow levelling rate... Although if we go off of that one Magikarp salesman who sells them for 500 PokÃ© a pop, farmers would make an absolute killing by breeding them! He's also known to be a massive conman but I don't know of any other referenced Magikarp prices. Goldeen could be a viable alternative, with a slower hatch time compensated by a faster levelling rate and average weight, but it depends if the people of PokÃ©mon are that keen on goldfish meat. With this considered, youâ€™d have a lot more factors going into which bird PokÃ©mon is the most efficient to raise, sell, and eat

Personally, I like the concept that pokemon and real-life animals live along with eachother.
Science
it's not just hatching and growing, it's also about domestication and behaviour: a wild animal or one that's particularly difficult to keep under control likely isn't worth it. So you should actually take a step back and consider how likely it would be for a certain pokemon to be domesticated, and whether it would be well behaved enough.  One factor that is really important in domestication is how well the animal/pokemon is able to convert things which we can't digest, easily eat ourselves, or don't want to eat (although modern farming practices have gone beyond this efficient process into inefficiency of active feeding with things we can eat, or things grown on fields where we could grow human food) -Pigs are known for eating a lot of our food scraps. -Cows can graze pastures of grass which we don't eat ourselves. -Chickens grains which fall on the ground during traditional processing of grains and might be hard to all pick back up for human use.  Multiple uses out of one animal would've traditionally also been a factor in which animals get domesticated: -goats and sheep produce milk, meat, fur/wool, and leather -horses can be used for meat and leather, but also are very useful in pulling carts and ploughs and all that, and the meat tends to be more a by-product once they're not useful for their main function anymore. -Chickens provide eggs, and later meat and feathers

some additional pokemon facts you may or may not have found during research:  Mudsdale is such a massive outliers weight-wise because it is among the few pokemon that are properly scaled in terms of height and weight to their real life counterparts, in this case being clydesdale horses. wailord, amusingly, is also in this group for one of its inspirations; it's not a scarily lightweight whale, it's a whale-shaped blimp that somehow lives underwater in spite of being way less dense than water.   hoothoot may not be the best choice for a bird pokemon as food, as it actually has 2 legs it swaps between when hopping. this, combined with the fact that its feathers are thick enough to make this hard to notice, means that most of its excess body weight relative to its counterparts likely consists of foot and feather, rather than enjoyable meat.   some pokemon have edible body parts that can be taken without harming the animal. applins make their homes inside of an apple, rather than actually being an apple. they have thin, green, wormlike bodies, so wanting to eat an apple with what amounts to a dragon type, hermit crab acting worm is debatable, but you can eat from an applin without hurting it. you would just be evicting it. the banana-like fruit that makes up a tropius' beard and the eggs held by chansey and blissey are all edible, and are freely given to those the pokemon like. they all grow back. eating a slowpoke tail is a fair bit less ethical, as it's still a body part of a living creature, but it does also grow back and doesn't do much lasting harm to the thing outside of stopping shellder from biting it there. and probably some psychological trauma.   after giving it some thought, its entirely possible that the team rocket branch in mahogany town was forcing magikarps to evolve early not to make powerful pokemon for their use, but as a way to make easily accessible meat for sale. a 5th of the standard step count to hatch, some radio waves to evolve it, and now you have 500 pounds of newborn, but very pissed off meat, which is a fair bit easier to... process... than if it had lived any meaningful length of time to learn how the world works and was still 500 pounds and very pissed off. they were also shown in the same game to be willing to resort to unethical food sales for profit, what with the slowpokes in azalea.

My main question about this is why fish were immediately discounted. While it may make up a comparatively small amount of western diets, PokÃ©mon is a Japanese property, and fish make up around 40% of protein in the average Japanese diet. (I haven't compared all fish Pokemon, Feebas and Goldeen come to mind as contenders, but went with Magikarp as a default)   Considering that Magikarp have the fastest hatch rate and require little space and care (going off of real life fish farming processes and Magikarp jump of all games) I'd assume they make up a significant part of the market. Granted the average weight of 22lb/10kg isn't great, alongside the slow levelling rate... Although if we go off of that one Magikarp salesman who sells them for 500 PokÃ© a pop, farmers would make an absolute killing by breeding them! *He's also known to be a massive conman but I don't know of any other referenced Magikarp prices.*  Goldeen could be a viable alternative, with a slower hatch time compensated by a faster levelling rate and average weight, but it depends if the people of PokÃ©mon are that keen on goldfish meat.   After all that though, you could argue that Magikarp and other fish PokÃ©mon would be part of the fishing market rather than farming, hence disqualifying them from this video. You should check Brian David Gilbert's analysis of which Pok[emon is the most edible
Not sure if you'll ever revisit this topic but there's a couple of things you haven't considered for this hypothetical question and the most important thing you hadn't considered was taste. An animal's diet can alter the flavor of its meat, so there really is a difference between grass fed and grain fed meat but the most important thing about this fact is that the meat of predatory terrestrial animals tends to taste horrible. Hoothoot, being an owl Pokemon, a predatory bird, would actually taste pretty bad and even if raised on an insect diet would probably be an inefficient livestock option compared to an omnivorous or herbivorous bird. Additionally, as mentioned plenty of times in the comments, leveling growth does not determine maturity, for example it's mentioned in some of Kangaskhan's Pokedex entries that it takes 3 years for its babies to become mature enough to start walking outside the mother's pouch. But for the sake of simplicity (which I'm sure is why the video uses level growth rates), let's continue using growth rates. You said evolving Pokemon would be inefficient but as it turns out, thanks to Sword and Shield and its Pokemon Camp feature, it turns out Pokemon are capable of leveling up by just being well cared for. I could imagine some farms put in that extra effort and cost to raise livestock that do evolve, the Pokemon world's equivalent of non-factory farming, so Gogoat and Mudsdale could believably be on the table. Granted, I bet a lot of Mudsdale's weight is actually from the hardened mud all over its hair and hooves.

8:37 That would be the case however the growth rate does not necessarily refer to the rate at which a Pokemon grows in age. A Pokemon's age and the Pokemon's level are not necessarily directly linked to one another. As time passes the Pokemon gets older and the more time they are battling the faster they level up. They both are dependent on time.   We know that level and age are not necessarily directly linked, because one refers to how much the time the Pokemon has lived while the other refers to how much of life they spent training. In the anime there is an actual old Pokemon, it is an old Treeko. If levels and age are directly linked then the Treeko should be a pretty dang high level to look as old as it seems, or maybe it just kept rejecting to evolve however the only time this has been known to be possible if a trainer cancels the evolution.   On the other hand even if the growth rate referred to the rate at which it grew it would still not fit with what we know of pokemon. Because even if we level up a Pokemon the weight and height does not change, this of course I am referring to the main line series games where the data of the Pokemon is always shown. The Pokemon Go and side line games I am not sure about though. But if we take what we know about the Mainline games and the anime, this theory utilizing the exp growth rate system does not hold ground for the speed at which a Pokemon grows.

I think your dairy calculations are off. Yes, Miltank grows more slowly than Skiddo. But it learns the move Milk Drink at level 11(in generation 5, since we'recomparing directly with Skiddo). Since that move can be used in-game to heal other pokemon in your party, it can be assumed that it's ready to be milked at or soon after that age. Skiddo never learns the move, perhaps implying that it, like goats IRL, take more labor to produce their milk. While a level 1 Skiddo is theoretically sexually mature, they're definitely nowhere near their milk-producing peak. I think it's more likely that milking starts when they evolve at level 35. The ranches we see in the anime have Gogoats, so I think we can assume that evolution is in play. Additionally, Skiddo are only about 31 kg, while Miltank are 75.5 kg, and Gogoat are  91 kg. Mass probably isn't a direct translation to milk production, but there's got to be a correlation. And since using Miltank's Milk Drink to heal other pokemon depletes Miltank's HP, I think HP can be said to correlate, too. Miltank's base HP stat is 95, Skiddo's is 66, and Gogoat's is 123.(You could also apply the Special Defense stat, since it tends to imply fatty bulk, good for making milk, but the distributionis similar, so there's little point.) So with the extra labor to get Skiddo and Gogoat milk, as well as the extra time and labor to raise them to evolution, there does seem to be a trade-off against Miltank's quicker maturity and easier milking (I don't know any animals in the real world who can walk up to a bucket and intentionally milk themselves, besides humans, but I imagine it'd be a serious saving in labor), the more wide-open pastures they seem to need may make for a similar trade-off to cows vs goats in the real world. And finally, all miltank are female. One male pokemon can impregnate as many miltank as we like, and will produce only other Miltank. But half the Skiddo born will be male, and thus unable to produce milk. In a region like Kalos, where quick and cheap transit is highly valued, those males aren't a total loss. But in less urban places like Johto, where distances are too great for a sprinter like Gogoat, half your stock will be a total loss.

The medium slow and medium fast are somewhat of a misnomer, as the medium fast group is actually slower in the early levels, they just take less XP to get to level 100, making them quicker in the late game. OMG, MatPat. The amount of misinformation is infuriating. The reason meat is so cheap in the U.S. is because of our tax dollars being used to subsidize the industry because of Beef Checkoff. Billions of upon billions of taxpayer dollars every year go towards beef, dairy, and eggs to keep it cheap.   The correct answer to this video is no Pokemon should be eaten. If you're going to do a real world comparison then the reasons you shouldn't eat Pokemon are:  Terrible for the environment. Eating animal products will ALWAYS be WAY less efficient than just eating those calories yourself. Why feed 20 - 50 pounds of food A DAY to a miltank when you could have just fed that same food to humans directly? Don't tell me humans can't eat wheat, soy, and corn. The IPCC said if everyone stopped eating animal products then by 2050 we'd be able to grow MORE food on LESS land and free up space to restore forests and other habitats to wildlife AND lower carbon emissions.  Zoonotic diseases. We've been in a freaking pandemic for nearly 2 years now because of zoonotic diseases. If the Pokemon world was more realistic, there would be influenza outbreaks every year because of people eating pidgeys or whatever farmed animal they decided to eat. Influenza mostly comes from chickens and kills tens of thousands of people every year. SARS, influenza, leukemia, bird flu, swine flu, mad cow disease, and many others come from animals. No one ever got the lentil flu. We get those diseases from ANIMALS not plants.  Health. You don't need/want protein from animals. It's terrible for your health. If Pokemon was the real world then in 2019, the #1 and #2 killers worldwide would be heart attack and stroke, respectively. Those are diet-related diseases. In the Pokemon world, 15 million people would have died because they were eating a crap diet and it caught up to them.  And then of course there is ethics. Most people are hypocritical when it comes to their love of animals. We're taught dogs and cats are awesome but it's fine to torture and murder these other animals over here. Cows are smarter than dogs and pigs are as intelligent as a 3 year old human child. Imagine putting a 3 year old human child into a gas chamber. Thatâ€™s what we do for bacon.  And you literally supported factory farming.   You're supposed to be a man of science. I understand getting past the idea you need to eat animals to get your protein or whatever is very difficult but it's utter crap. You're literally hurting your audience and don't even know it. I have loved all the real world science for Food Theory, but having it applied to Pokemon is always fun.
Why do we use specific animals for meat? Well, here's some fact fodder for ya... 1) We don't usually eat predators. While there are exceptions like snake, and the occasional omnivore like pig, most food animals are prey, like cow, chicken, sheep, deer, buffalo, etc. In fact, there are some predators we can't eat, like wolves who are so high in vitamin E that they're basically toxic if you eat enough. But here's the real bombshell: 2) We usually eat female animals. We eat cow, not bull. We eat chicken, not rooster. Again, there are exceptions like deer, where we eat both buck and doe, but the majority of food animals are female. Of course, part of that is about breeding, but it's also because many male animals, like bulls, have tough, unpalatable meat. Then there's all the eggs and milk we get from females.  This even extends to plants. Of course, plant gender is irrelevant for most veggies like root veggies and greens, but think about all of the "seeds" we eat. Grains, nuts, beans, legumes, and even fruits are all types of seed, all harvested from female plants.
